<content>Chapter 1 The Uprising of the Tai Assam is a melting pot of different races, ethnic groups and cultures. For centuries, the fertile land of the Brahmaputra Valley has attracted the attention of foreign migrants. This is the reason why people of different races and religions come here. The country&amp;#39;s fertile soil is home to the cultural ideologies of people from other parts of the country as well as Southeast Asia and Tibet. Each ethnic group has its own contribution to the larger Assamese culture today and their diverse cultural elements have enriched the field of Assamese culture. Among the ethnic groups that migrated to Assam, the Ahom made an incomparable contribution. This is evident from the fact that the name &amp;#39;Assam&amp;#39; eventually derived from the name &amp;#39;Aham&amp;#39; When the first group of Ahom migrants crossed the Patkai in 1228 AD, no one could have imagined that this would be their settlement. Here they brought technological changes such as wetland farming, and formed complex social and political organizations. It was able to unite all its ethnic groups and tribes to form a large national entity. Along with the heroes of medieval India, the Ahoms ruled Assam for about a hundred years.</content>

</page>

<content>The name of Chaolung Chu-Kapha, the founder of the Ahom dynasty among the Ahoms, will always remain high in the history of Assam. He will always be held in high esteem not only as the founder of adventurous expeditions and military strength, but also as the statesman who established the foundations and rules of the Ahom Empire. 2 Chu-ka-fa and followers were of Tai origin. They are a part of the larger Tai core. Their descendants are still known as Tai Ahom. They are descendants of the western part of the Tai. Various groups of Tai live in the main part of the north of Southeast Asia and its surrounding areas. For thousands of years, these Tai people have settled from Assam to Yunnan Province in southern China and from Bangkok to Vietnam. Tai and Khai: Proper naming: The proper ethnic names of &amp;#39;Tai&amp;#39; or &amp;#39;Thai&amp;#39; have been analyzed in different ways by different scholars, but all these theories have limitations. However, the word &amp;#39;Thai&amp;#39; is a later word and refers to &amp;#39;free&amp;#39; or &amp;#39;free people&amp;#39; The Thais who live in Thailand (formerly Shyam) have their own distinctive characteristics and traditions. In the seventh century, a Tai prince named Phra Ruang ruled Chankal (heaven). This was the kingdom of Jimme (Chiengmai). Phra Ruang was the Kartalia king of Cambodia, then a powerful kingdom in southern Indo-China. However, Phra Ruang declared Zinme&amp;#39;s independence and refused Cambodian submission. Then, in 538 AD, the Shamiz year began. Phra Ruang as the national commander of the Thais. However, his reign did not last long. After his death, his son became king and on this occasion the King of Cambodia (Jai Burman) Kiangzhen again</content>

It is worth mentioning that Phra Ruang has been recognized as a hero by several kings. Among them is King Ram Tham-heng of Thailand. He stamped the name of Fra Ruang on the coin. The fact that several powerful kings such as Indraditya and Ram Kham-heng followed in the footsteps of Phra Ruan and described him as a hero suggests the tendency of the Thais to free themselves from Cambodia. In that sense, the word &amp;#39;Thai&amp;#39; undoubtedly originated in the eighth century. Edward Gait&amp;#39;s A History of Assam also gives the meaning of the word Tai in this way. He believes that the word &amp;#39;Tai&amp;#39; means glorious and the Chinese used it to mean celestial or divine. Similarly, the Ahom Buranji describes the Tai as the inhabitants of heaven. Nai Likhit Huntrakul “The Historical Records of the Shamiz-Chinese Relations” believes that the title of &amp;#39;Tai&amp;#39; was given to a class of people who enjoyed considerable rights and privileges in ancient times He was the second son of the first emperor of China, Huang Tai (2697-2596 BC), and the son-in-law of the third emperor, Tuan-Yak (2513-2435 BC). However, in later times, other nobles were given this honor even though they did not have royal status. These include Chawangtai (minister), Chi-tai (viceroy), Tang-tai (under secretary), Pu-tai (provincial governor), Git-tai (provincial teacher), Pan-tai (provincial treasurer), Tien tai (provincial commander) and Tao tai (regional governor). In the late nineteenth and early twentieth centuries, several scholars suggested that the Tai originated in China. According to this view, the Chinese acquired knowledge of their language, culture, science, arts and organization from the Agro-Altaic and Bok peoples who came from West Asia in the 23rd century BC. From that point of view, the terrain suggests that the Shan people were located in the Qilung Mountains, north of Jen-chuan in China and south of Chenxi. Dr. Gribson also believes that the original habitat of the Tai is north of the Yangtze and Huangho in north-western China. According to La Copari, Chinese language, art, science, and elements of social structure were acquired from the Agra-Altec Bok people from West Asia. The acquisition of these elements required the help of highly educated and cultured people, and the Chinese were assisted in this by the neighboring Susians. These teachings were accompanied by the imprint of Babylonian civilization and were reformed. La Copari said the young Chinese were strong and powerful and were able to drive out the Bok or Pehshing (name for Chinese immigrants) people who migrated to China and dominate the country. Tai People: Uprising and Migration As mentioned above, there is no reliable source in history or archeology to prove that the Tai are of Mongolian origin. There is also no reliable evidence that the Tai lived within a thousand miles of Mongolia or in the Altai Mountains. Secondly, the Tai people live in summer-dominated and rainfed cropland. Their traditional houses are decorated tall. Therefore, they cannot settle in desert-like climates like northern China or Mongolia. Scholars who have looked at the physical structure and heredity of the Tai and the Astro-Asians have been forced to admit that they have physical similarities to the A-Asians, not the Chinese. Perhaps only itinerant European historians sought to prove this idea in the middle of this century. Prince Damrong, on the other hand, refuses to accept this idea as true. In 1914, he wrote that only European historians tried to give the impression that the Tai were inhabitants of Nan Chau. However, there is no historical source. In 1885, La Copari wrote an article entitled The Cradle of the Shan Race, which stated that the Nan Chau Empire was founded by the Thais. His new theory had a far-reaching impact and even made its way into textbooks in countries such as Thailand. After extensive research, a Chinese historian named Zhang Jil found that the Bai people living in Dali and Lake Erhai were closely related to the Baiman (or Heman or Ehaman) people who lived in Nan-Chau and Dali during the Tang and Chang dynasties. In other words, the Hemans of the Nan Chau and Dali empires are the ancestors of the Bai people. They have settled in the border area of southeastern China for nearly a thousand years and have contributed to the economy and culture of the area. Most of the high-ranking officials of Nan-Chau belong to the Baiman tribe. The kings and councilors of the Dali Empire belonged to the same ethnic group. He added that the Thai people have many differences in history, language, traditions and religion with this ethnic group. Therefore, Zhang Jilo comments that the Nan Chau Empire was not established by the Thais. His view is supported by another Chinese scholar, Hu Hura Seng. The Tai have more genealogical ties to the Kadai and Indonesians than to the Chinese or Tibetan Burmans. However, this sequence has changed dramatically under Chinese influence. The migration of the Tai to the equatorial zone or to the Yunnan plain did not come from the north, but from the former wetlands and the coast of southern China. Current Tai-related studies and research tend to focus on the coastal areas as opposed to the idea that Sezwan and Yunnan were the earliest habitats of the Tai. <page_no> 14  </page_no>
<content>The Tai people&amp;#39;s entire history testifies to this, not during their migration to Southeast Asia. 14 However, the main and only reason for the migration of the Tai people was their refusal to accept the domination of the Han people. There are two main explanations for this – the first is demographic and the second is political. Initially, the demographics of the Han people did not influence the Tai until the century BC, but population growth at the end of this century made it necessary for them to produce monsoon crops to meet their food demands. For this they had to advance in search of flat land in the river valley. As it progressed, Tai culture developed among other ethnic groups and created a new cultural trend throughout Southeast Asia. They crossed many rivers when they migrated to the Indo-Chinese region. Their passage was facilitated by the Mekong and Saluin rivers. Crossing the Mekong, they established a small kingdom in northern Thailand and then another on the banks of the Chau Phraya River. The Tai, on the other hand, who migrated to the Chalui Na River Valley, adopted a system of rule modeled on the Shan Dynasty. The group that migrated to the Mekong River Valley split into several groups and moved south of the Mekong to the Chong Shu-Thai region of Tonkin. This region is in the southwest of northern Thailand. However, details of the history of the Tai people date back to the thirteenth century. The Tai chiefs established their rule in the river valleys and established their kingdom in the northern part of what is now Laos. They also settled in the southern part of the Mekong. of the borders of the Khmer Empire in northern Thailand at the time</content>

</page>

<content>Coup d&amp;#39;etat 15 They were able to advance southward due to weak rule. They soon gained political supremacy in what is now Thai Legu. The valleys of the Indus, Irrawaddy and Indus also gradually came under their control and they still dominate these places. Their migration to where the Tai people now live took place only in the thirteenth century. One group still dominates the fertile regions of the Brahmaputra Valley and the other the plains of Kwangchi. The territory inhabited by the Tai cannot be called a plain. Most of Unjani Lao and southern Burma were surrounded by high mountains and rivers, such as the Gyanjuri. That is probably why they could not come directly to this area. They had to come here through China. These mountains also prevented them from returning to China. It was not possible to maintain any link between the two Tai tribes settled in the Kwangxi Valley and the Tonkin Valley. Similarly, communication between the Tai tribes living in the Lao region, the Chinduin Valley and the Irrawaddy Valley was cut off. From the above discussion, it can be seen that the view that the Tai revolted off the coast of southeastern China is well-founded. The theory that they were the earliest dynasty in China is not credible. In the eyes of modern scholars, the Tai are actually inhabitants of the Yangtze River Valley and have linguistic and cultural similarities with the tribes of this valley.</content>

<content>CHAPTER II Socio-Political Context of Thirteenth Century Kamrup and Significance of the Arrival of Chaolung Chu-ka-pha Before the arrival of Chaolung Chu-ka-pha, East Kamrup was under the tribal kingdoms. The Chutia kingdom established its capital at Shadia, east of the Sovansiri and Dichang and possibly along the banks of the Dikhou and Brahmaputra rivers. The Hirimbial Kacharis, one of the strongest branches of the Kacharis to the west of the Dikhou, ruled the kingdom with their capital at Dimapur on the banks of the Dhansiri. The kingdom of the Hirimbial Kacharis extended from the east of the Dikhou to the Kalang or south of the Brahmaputra and to the Dhansiri valley and the northern Kachar hills. Another one of the strongest branches of the Kacharis is the Dimasa Kacharis. They were completely different from the Boro Kacharis of today&amp;#39;s southern Assam. The Dimasa king Bicharpatipha, a contemporary of Tu-ka-pha, was known to be the son of the god Shiva. His kingdom extended to Mohang in the east, but Chu-ka-fai drove him to the hills east of Barhat and south of Jaipur and conquered his kingdom. The neighboring kingdoms of Charideo were the Maran and Barahi. Their total population was about four thousand and their kingdom was south of Charing and north of the kingdom of the Kacharis. They are the Marans</content>

</page>

<page>
<page_no> 19  </page_no>
<content>The socio-political of Kamrup in the 19th century then ruled the region bounded by the Budhi Dihing to the north, the Dichang to the south, the Chafrai to the east and the Brahmaputra to the west. Although they were scattered in this region, they were united under the leadership of their tribal chief Badaucha with their capital as their chief administrative centre. Chu-ka-fai faced severe resistance from some of the Marans but later he was able to conquer them through both forced repression and peaceful efforts. The Maran people were called Matak by Chu-ka-fai and Matak means &amp;#39;powerful man&amp;#39; in Tai language. While West Kamrup had a remarkable history of a kind of harmony between upper castes and tribals, the tools available for reconstructing the history of ancient Assam (Pragajyotish or Kamrup) are confusing and disorganized; However, it is known from the Ramayana and Mahabharata that the ancient Pragajyotish kingdom was much wider than the geographical area of modern Assam and certainly included Sylhet. The context of the Ramayana shows that the kingdom of Pragajyotisha extended to the Southern Sea. This fact is also supported by the Mahabharata. However, the true political history of ancient Assam begins with the kings of the Burman dynasty. Pushyavarman, the successor of the Varman dynasty of Narak Bhagadatta, was the first king of this dynasty. Therefore, the Burman dynasty probably began at least in the first century BC. In that sense, Narak and Bhagadatta actually established the first historical dynasty at that time. All the rulers of that period were the heirs of Narak and Bhagadatta until the end of the Pala dynasty. However, we cannot identify a direct relationship between the first Narak and Bhagadatta, although the latter was probably the son of the last Narak, who was a distant relative of the Bhouma dynasty founded by Narak. There are several references to hell in history, among them the Kalika Purana</content>

<content>The thirteenth century socio-political legend of Kamarupa included Naraka, a contemporary of Janaka and Banasura. During the reign of Bhutivarma, who ruled Kamarupa in the fifth century 20 BC, a significant attempt was made to Aryanize about three hundred Brahmins of various tribes in this border region. (References Bhutivarma&amp;#39;s Bar Ganga inscription, Harshacharita, Bhaskar Varma&amp;#39;s Nidhanpur copper plate). Various inscriptions and copper plates of the Bhoum Narak dynasty clearly show that although the local kings were Bhumiputra tribal kings and most of their subjects were tribal, they were highly Aryanized and became worshipers of the god Shiva and Goddess Parvati. They even patronized Brahmins from other parts of India. During the reign of Bhaskarvarma, Gaur was conquered and the lost glory of Kamarupa was restored to some extent (context: Nidhanpur copper plate of Bhaskarvarma). He made an alliance with the powerful king Harshavardhana which led to a link with the upper castes of Kanauj and a large number of them came to settle in Kamarupa. The Brahmins came to Assam mainly on their own initiative and in some cases at the invitation of the king who provided them with flexible facilities to stay in Assam. Most of the inscriptions before the thirteenth century dealt with the donation of land to Brahmins. Apart from the Brahmins, two other Aryan-speaking communities of the Caucasian ethnic group, the Kalitas and Kayastha, probably also migrated to Assam during the early AD century. The Brahmin community, on the other hand, was a follower of the strict Hindu system of worship and as most of them had migrated from Bengal, their religious practice was mainly influenced by the Bengal Brahmin system of worship. The main deities of the Ahom in religious activities are Landon, Lankuri, Jachingpha, Ai Leng Din, Khaokham, Janchai Hung, Lauthri, Pujakji, Chitkamcham, etc. Along with these, there are also the sun gods (Khunban) and the moon gods (Khun-dun). The Ahom also believe in an almighty God (whom they call Fa Tu Ching from Hum). The religious festivals of the Ahom were Umpha, Me-Dam Mephi, Furalung, Rikskhan, etc. There were no temples of the Ahom gods except Chomdeu. Chomdeu was the main idol of the Ahom kings and could only be touched by the kings. Along with Chu-ka-pha, the beliefs or concepts of the old men and the gods associated with mountains, rivers, etc. came to Assam. At that time, however, the Ahom had not come into contact with the Brahmins and therefore there was no attempt to identify the gods and goddesses of the Ahom and the Hindus. That work was done only towards the end of the Ahom Empire. Therefore, there was no conflict between Hindu and Ahom religions during the reign of Chu Ka-pha and the local people could practice their religion fearlessly and impartially. Another great contribution of Chu-ka-pha was in agriculture. Chu-ka-fa was very skilled in irrigation. He was well aware of the aspects to be considered when selecting land for permanent agriculture and settlement, as evidenced by his measurement that the weight of the southern stream of the Dikhou River was twice that of the lower stream of the Dikhou River. He did not stay everywhere when he went abroad to Habung, Dimau, Shimluguri etc. and seeing the muddy ground, he did not stay in Timon. Because he saw the possibility of flooding. <content>The socio-political of Kamrup in the thirteenth century.... and a total of three khats (crop fields) were opened in Angera. and employed a large number of Marans in excavation. At that time, the Assamese people did not know the method of rice cultivation except in Barbhuiya in West Assam and on the north bank of the Brahmaputra. The Barbhuiyans plowed with cows in irrigation, did not use oxen and were not aware of irrigation methods. Khuka-fai saw that the people living in Souma Peeth (Maran, Barahi, Kachari, Chutia, Deuri, etc.) did not know how to plow and only sowed paddy by setting fire and cultivating jhum. He saw that the Barahi and Maran people had spacious yards, fenced their houses and raised goats, pigs, chickens, ducks, etc. But they did not know the techniques of irrigation. So he introduced the method of irrigation and plowing with a ya mohai. He brought with him seeds of sticky Bora rice from his motherland and introduced it in Assam. This new agricultural method was easily adopted by the Maran and Barahi people and introduced irrigation. It revolutionized paddy cultivation in East Assam and the irrigation system as well as the irrigation system by building small alleys in the fields gained great popularity by awakening the innovative power of the local farmers. They started raising me and cows for irrigation as the local tribal people did not eat cow or cow milk. Thus, Chu-ka-fai changed the nature of rural life in Eastern Assam and provided a simple livelihood for the people which paved the way for a prosperous Assam. His Majesty Chu-ka-fai and even his Prime Minister Buragohain married Barahi and Maran girls according to necessity. There is no Magh Bihu among the Maran and Barahi people during the reign of Chu-ka-pha, which suggests that this festival is also a contribution of the Ahom people. The Tai state Chu-ka-fai introduced the ancient system of rule of the Tai people in southern Burma and southern China when he came to Assam. As the bearer of her tradition, Chu-ka-fai appointed a chief administrator in each of the regions he occupied. Probably in accordance with her policy of administrative division, she also introduced the system of governing the administrative system of Assam by dividing it into regions, provinces or districts. Each province or district appointed an administrator called Rajkhowa (Fukin Mu). The administrative system he introduced was a kind of monarchy in which only a male member of the royal family and the children of the king could claim the throne. The administrative system was organized on the basis of succession with the king followed by the council of ministers (Burgohai, Bargohai). After the cabinet were the chief administrators of the provinces. The administrative system of the cabinet is similar to that prevailing in the eastern Maulung or Mummit and other Tai states. After the death of the king in the absence of sons, the council of ministers administers the kingdom until a prince of the same lineage can be appointed to the throne according to the tradition. Throughout the Ahom rule, there was never a situation where ministers had to sit on the throne because they</content>

Hu Ka Fai introduced this characteristic system of rule which continued to the end. The administrative system was ubiquitous and even officers were appointed to carry out administrative work down to the grassroots. The Ahom had no permanent army but a special system was established for every healthy man to perform compulsory military service. This system enabled the young men to remain healthy and ready for battle at all times. Hu-ka-fai introduced gunpowder and cannons from Maulung to the martial arts of Assam. The kings of Kamarupi did not know how to use bartops and gunpowder. They used only bows, arrows, knives, sticks and elephants. The introduction of gunpowder and cannons revolutionized martial arts. In terms of clothing and jewellery, Chu-ka-fai introduced the first shoes and nara cholas (Tai clothes). The jewelery he brought with him included jangfai, jonbiri, various pearls, etc. and the costumes included kingkhap, Ahom mekhela, various velvet dresses, etc., and the utensils included maihang, banbati, bankanhi, etc. Another notable contribution of Chu-ka-pha to Assamese culture is the Hengdang. In the days of the Kamarupi kings, it was not customary to record the flow of royal events. Therefore, we have to rely on inscriptions, copperplates etc. for sources of history. Ghu-ka-fai introduced the first practice of writing history. Ahom history tells us that Chu-ka-fai ordered the scholars of the Kham Yang kingdom he had established to record the truth of everything the king said, did and saw and to continue this practice. There is nothing unusual in the fact that the Assamese nation can be proud of its historical literature as there is no other example of systematic history writing in the entire country like Assam. Hu-ka-fa&amp;#39;s most important contribution was the social and religious harmony he advocated. He never wanted to impose religion on his subjects or force them to adopt a language. His initiatives in this regard were reflected in the generosity, religious and social tolerance shown by the Ahom kings during these hundred years of Ahom rule, and the Assamese language developed as a common language and the people united as an Assamese nation. When Chu-Ka-Fa came to Assam, the Assamese nation had not even sprouted and people identified themselves by caste and tribe instead of caste. There is a lot of information in the Ahom history about how Chu-ka-fai united the people of East Assam and paved the way for the formation of the Assamese nation. First he made alliances with the kings of Maran and Barahi by marrying their daughters. They forced the Marans who refused to accept their rule to accept it by force. Others made alliances and some of them were included in the Ahom clan. While living in Tipam near the Dihing River, he was attracted to a Matak who supplied him with eggplant. Hu-ka-fai named it Lanmakhru (eggplant mother in Ahom language). Lanmadhuru also helped in many ways during the war with the Nagas. In reward, Chu-ka-fai included him in the Ahom clan. After his death, his son Chao-i-jip (Tao-i-jep-tat-fa of Tamuliphukan and I-dep-tat-fa of Nei Elias) ascended the throne in 638 AD and reigned for forty years. The new era of the famous Ai Zep Lakni or Mao Chan began in the year Chao Ai Zep ascended the throne. I. Jeppe died in 678 AD. The throne was vacant as he had no heir. Therefore, the ministers and officials went to Chao-Khun-Jun (Khuchur), the ruler of Mu-Kho-Mujao, and told him everything and asked him to send a prince of his descendants to rule the kingdom. Chao-khun-jun sent his son Kham-pong-pha there. After reigning for five years, he died in 703 AD, leaving his eldest son Kham Tap-pha as his heir. 80</content>

In the same year, Kham Tappha&amp;#39;s brother Kham Typ-fai-mu-mao-lung took over and established his capital at Ma Kao Mu Lung (Maolung). (1: Introductory sketch of the History of the Shans: N.Elias: 1876) But according to Ne Elias, Kham Tap Fai (Elias Kamchip Fa) succeeded his brother in 703 AD and established his capital at Mao-Kao-Mu-Lung, boycotted Mu-bi-sung-ram as his capital. Historically, Chao Ijeg ascended the throne in 638 AD and ruled for 40 years. Thus, in the seventh century, the first significant succession of Tai power occurred in the Mekong Valley and southern Burma. Dr. Kuching points out that the Tai people migrated from the Yushman Mountains in the south to the Nam-Chao (Shelley River) Valley and its adjacent areas in the sixth century AD. Almost the entire Shelley Valley became the home of the Shans and the seat of political power. From this region the Tai extended to the north, west and southeast. Thus not only the valley but the entire region between the Mekong and the Irrawaddy, including the Shan states of Burma, came under the control of the Tai group known as the Mao-Khan. However, this is found only in the histories of the Shans, which are not entirely accurate and in some cases unreliable. The significance of I-jep-lakni is that it gives a historical date for the coronation of Chao I-zep, son of Ju Lai, as king of Mu-bi-murom. “The Ahom history is characterized by the accession of the first Ahom king Mukafa to the throne in 1228 AD</content>

In doing so, they left behind two quotes, either of which can be considered the beginning. . The first thing is that astronomers and other scholars calculated that eleven Tao Chinga (ages) had passed from the time Khulung-Khun Lai descended from heaven and established the kingdom until Chukapha ascended the throne of southern Assam. The second is that these Burmese people began their national era during the reign of the Mau king Ai Jep (or Ai Jep-Tatpha). Now if we subtract one Tao-Chinga or 660 years from 1228 AD, ie from the time of Chukapha&amp;#39;s accession to the throne, we get 568 AD The rule of I-Jep, initiated by the Shans, began in the 70th year of the establishment of Muri Muram (568+70=638 AD). <content>Chaolung Chu-ka-fa, the history of his successors... , was succeeded by his son Chu-khang-fa. After conquering the original inhabitants of the area where they settled, the population of the Ahom increased unexpectedly in the peaceful environment that prevailed for a long time. Such population growth cannot be attributed to natural processes alone; The integration of the indigenous people into the Ahom, and probably new migrations from the original settlements of the Ahom, also contributed to this growth. However, the growth of the population now brought them to a position where they could stand alone against the powerful kings around them. As a result, through several battles, they became an indispensable force in the entire Brahmaputra Valley. In December 1667, the Mughal Emperor of Delhi appointed Raja Ram Singh, son of Mirza Raja Jai Singh of Amber, as commander of an army reinforced by the Bengal army. Ram Singh&amp;#39;s army was well equipped with 18,000 cavalry, 15,000 archers and 30,000 infantry. The Barphukan of Guwahati also made all necessary preparations to confront the Mughals. The plan of defense was prepared in consultation with the three ministers, the Fukans and the Rajkhowas. Ram Singh soon ordered the evacuation of Guwahati and the entire kingdom west of the Barnadi. Barphukan rejected Ram Singh&amp;#39;s demands and advanced towards Agiathuri with his army and ordered Fukan and other officers to attack the enemy. There were several battles and finally the Mughal army was defeated. Ram Singh attacked the Ahom army with his Rajput cavalry and killed 10,000,000 Assamese soldiers in one day. This battle is known in the history of Assam as the “Battle of Alabai”. .</content>

<page_no> 89  </page_no>
<content>The method of converting the language and script of the Ahom into symbols will not preserve the syntax of the original Ahom language; Instead, an artificial language will be created. The Ahom people would benefit from establishing the Ahom words and phrases found in reliable Ahom sources on the basis of the pronunciation rules of the law language. Now it is necessary to discuss some things about the &amp;#39;type&amp;#39; or size and font of the printing press of the Ahom language. The Ahom-Assamese English Dictionary, published by the Baptist Mission Press in Calcutta in 1920, was the first printed script in the Ahom language. The Ahom Buranji (1930) and The Ahom Primer (1936) and The Ahom Lexicon (1964) also use the same printed script. In 1987, a new font was introduced for the publication of the Assamese Engish-Tai Dictionary and it is much different from the font used in the 1920s. In 1992, Trewill and Ranu invented a new type. Stephen More came up with another type of type. The Eastern Tai Sahitya Sabha has a heavy responsibility in this regard. They should develop a font based on the letters and words used in the unpublished books in handwriting. This will open a new path for the Ahom language and script. So this should be done as soon as possible. Context Notes : (1) This scholar, B. J. Trawill, has given extensive discussion of the Ahoms in two books, &amp;#39;The Tai of Assam&amp;#39; (2) Dr. J. N. Fukan in his article &amp;#39;Ahom Script in the Thirteenth Century&amp;#39; comments on the Ahom script and gives specific examples of consonant consonants to prove the fact that the Ahom script was brought by Chu-ka-kai in the early thirteenth century.</content>

<page_no> 90  </page_no>
<content>The Ahom Language and Script (3) Article by BJ Terwiel. It should be noted. Researchers Alfons Weidert and Stephen Morey of the University of Heidelberg studied the reconstruction of the tone system of the Ahom language. They comment that the linguistic classification of Ahom within the northwestern dialect group of Tai languages (which includes Khamti and Shan) is modern in converting the vowels at the beginning of words in pro-Tai languages to non-vowels or non-vowels Based on the tendencies of the Tai languages. Like Khamti and Shan, Khun, Tai Yuan and Tailu, which Egerod classified between the Shan and Siamese language groups in 1959, have a tendency to convert the original vowels to non-vowels. On the other hand, the Ahom language converts the vowels of the original Pro-Tai language (like Siamese) to nasal consonants, as seen in Khamti and Shan. He thinks that the differences between Ahom, Khamti and Shan on the one hand, and Siamese and their dialects on the other, are not so important as to justify dividing the Tai languages into separate subgroups. It is also argued that the etymological identification of the written form of the Siamese language with the vocabulary of the Ahom language is based on the phonetic similarity of the words Therefore, hundreds of Ahom words can be limited to three phonetic characteristics *A, *B, *C, with the help of the two phonetic symbols shown in the Siamese script. (Journal of the German Orinted Academy 1979) In this way, linguists can carry out the process of reconstruction of the tone characteristic system of the Ahom language. However, in doing so, the law and the tone characteristics of the language must also be considered.</content>

AHW Bentick was then the Commissioner of the Assam Valley. He was appointed the unpaid Provincial Director of the department. The importance of establishing this department for research and study of history alone cannot be underestimated. The government did see that the work of the Department of Ethnography had increased significantly and it was completely preoccupied with the research and practice of ethnology of various ethnic groups and had no time for historical research. This is the reason why the government had to open the Department of History and Archaeology. In terms of ethnology, both published many books on hill tribes such as Khasi, Mikir, Kachari, Luchai-Kuki, Meitei (in the days of Gordon) and Chema, Angami, Lotha and Ao Naga. <content>CHAPTER VIII The Religion of the Ahom The Ahom brought with them from Myung Maolung when they entered Assam under the leadership of Tai Yuvraj Su-ka-pha, the founder of the Ahom kingdom and ancestor of the Ahom dynasty I think it is appropriate to present an analysis of the scriptures. It can be said with certainty that the most important scripture of the Ahom people is &amp;#39;Lit Lai Pak Pen Ka Ka&amp;#39; This book can also be called the Bible of the Ahom people. <content>160 Ahom Religion Furalung is the worship of the virtues and righteous principles of life and values the honesty and dignity of man. Legend has it that Chaolung Du-ka-fai brought the Furalung scripture (Nom Mang Phura) with him and that Tengrai Mo&amp;#39;sai, son of the prominent scholar Senka Luk Mo Sai, wrote the Furalung in the fourteenth century during the reign of his son Chu-teo-pha. This scripture says that in the beginning Phura had no form or shape but shone like a bright flame in the vacuum. This scripture also mentions the lighting of one hundred and one lanterns, and it is said that even Fa-nuru-Langdon praises the act of burning one hundred and one slums on the high altar. During the Furalung Puja, the holy scripture &amp;#39;Pak Pen Ka Ka&amp;#39; is placed on the altar and 101 lanterns are lit. A very holy atmosphere is created and all people pray to Almighty God (Fura) to deliver them from sorrow, misfortune and evil intentions and grant them peace In short, Phuralung Puja means paying homage to the god Phura, worshiping the great Alang. It is clearly stated that Fura is implicit in the holy Pak Pen Ka Ka and Fura is the enlightening power. Therefore, the worship should be performed in a very humble manner and without any bloody sacrifices. The Rik Khun book contains detailed descriptions of the rituals required for the Rik Khan (prolonging life). This religious ceremony is held from house to house for the welfare of the family but is also celebrated collectively. Rik-khan was celebrated at different times during the reign of the Ahom kings The king held this ceremony publicly at the time of the coronation of the king or when the king won a battle, or when there was a prophecy of evil. Then he burns the feathers of the chicken, rips open the chest, and removes the veiny eyebrows. The chicken is then roasted in soybean oil, salt and finely chopped ginger and offered to the gods on a high altar. The chicken is offered with a basket of rice and boiled eggs. The chicken is also given some rice as a symbol of feeding. The Ahom priests offer these offerings to the gods with the appropriate mantras. After this ceremony, the priests immerse the gods (baitang). After the immersion, the worshipers and guests are treated to puja bhog and drinks. The priests usually sit on palm leaves to perform this puja. The worshipers (ie family members or those who are offering worship) are not given anything to sit on. They should be on their knees to pray. It tells how Umpha Puja first came into being. In order to establish unity and harmony between the Ahom and non-Ahom peoples of his kingdom like Maran, Barahi, Chutia etc., Chaolung Chu-Kapha introduced this puja by including the traditional Ahom gods and goddesses as well as the gods and goddesses worshiped by the local people. In addition to the Ahom gods worshiped in Umpha Puja, local gods and goddesses and marginal gods are worshipped. In this puja, white calves/cows are offered (sacrificed) to the Ahom deity Fa-Nuru-Landon and chickens, goats, pigs, dogs, bulls, ducks etc. are sacrificed to the local gods and goddesses. When this puja is done only for the welfare of a family/householder, only the goddesses Ain- Bhani, Saru Bauli, Bar Bauli, Aghari Bauli and Dongdangia, Gosani are worshipped. Gosani is given different names in different places, such as Lakhim in the paddy barn, Nang Chichao in the backyard garden, Gosani on the roadside, Subchini inside the house and the wife of the water god Khaokham on the banks of the river. This rule clearly shows that the Ahoms were influenced by Shakti Puja or Devi Puja when they first arrived in Assam as the ancient inhabitants of the country, mainly Chutia, Barahi, Maran and Kachari, worshiped Kechaikhati or Budhi Gosani, the most powerful goddess of the region. Thus, the Ahom scriptures contain detailed instructions on their traditional religious customs. However, the subtle changes in the religious practices prevalent among the Ahom can only be known by personal observation of the actual methods and detailed methods of observing these rituals. The religious concepts associated with these customs and the obligation to observe them in practice formed the basis of the religious philosophy of the Ahoms. In this study, we would like to focus on the actual methods of observing these religious practices as they are practiced today. Since these customs are actually practiced in the households of the Tai Ahom priests, we will focus on the religious rituals performed in their households. In this regard, the Ahom priestly community (Mahung, Changbun, Maplong ie Mohan, Deodhai, Bailung) determines the rituals of various pujas/festivals on the basis of ancient scriptures. In truth, they are the ones who have been strictly observing these customs. Other Tai Ahom people can also serve as priests for ceremonies such as Me-Dam-Me-Fi and Chak-Long. But the Mohans, Deodhais and Bailungs are the truly strict religious leaders and their words are the final decision in all matters. Therefore, their place in the Ahom society is very important. <content>They do not perform religious ceremonies without sincere faith, even collectively. The following religious rituals and customs are traditionally celebrated in the Ahom villages, especially in the villages of Deodhai, Mohan and Bai Lung, and more and more people are becoming attracted to these rituals day by day. The most important religious festival widely celebrated among the Ahom is the Me-Dam-Me-Fi. We would like to discuss this important religious ceremony in two parts (1) Family or family-centric Dam Puja (worship of the ancestors of a family) and (2) Public Me-Dam-Me-Fi (public worship of all ancestors). It is called Dam Puja. In such Dam Puja, the dead god (Dam) of deceased parents, grandparents and great-grandparents is worshipped. The deceased god of the grandfather is called &amp;#39;Ghai Dam&amp;#39; (meaning the chief god) or &amp;#39;Chao Tung Dam&amp;#39; The dead god of the parents is called &amp;#39;Na-dam&amp;#39; (meaning new god) or &amp;#39;An-mou-dam&amp;#39; The ghost of the grandfather/grandfather is called &amp;#39;Chiren-Dam&amp;#39; and other ancestors are called &amp;#39;Jakrua Dam&amp;#39; or &amp;#39;Dam Thao Dam Bao Dam Likha Likhi&amp;#39; In the kitchen, located at the far north of the main residence of the Ahoms (the last room), the Ahom family erected the pillar or pole of Dam Puja according to traditional Ahom customs. This pillar is called &amp;#39;Dam-khuta (pole of the ancestral dead gods) or &amp;#39;Pho-kam pillar. The Mahans established the pillars in the west and the Deodhai Bailuts in the east.</content>

<content>166 Ahom legend has it that an ancestor of the Mahans was a descendant of the moon god (Khuntun) and an ancestor of the Deodhais was a descendant of the sun god (Khuban). The Mahans sleep on the bed with their heads facing west and the Deodhais with their heads facing east to avoid stepping on their respective dam poles. After the &amp;#39;Tilni&amp;#39; ceremony three days later and the &amp;#39;Daha&amp;#39; ceremony ten days later, the deceased god or dam of the head of the household is installed on the ancestral pillar or pole. On the tenth day after death, the dead god of the new generation is established as Na-Dam by killing a female chicken (p kari) with a branch of a honey tree and offering other items for the purpose of Na-Dam. There is a folk belief among the Ahom that there are two kinds of human souls ; One of them is called &amp;#39;Pu&amp;#39; and the other is called &amp;#39;Fu or &amp;#39;Han&amp;#39; The consciousness of the soul that the child receives from the father while in the womb is called &amp;#39;pu&amp;#39; On the other hand, when the child is exposed to air, soil, water, light and sound at birth, the &amp;#39;fu&amp;#39;ba&amp;#39; or &amp;#39;han&amp;#39; form of the soul appears. The two forms of the soul meet at birth. However, as soon as a person dies, the &amp;#39;pu&amp;#39; form of the soul takes up residence in the &amp;#39;dam-khuta&amp;#39; and is eligible for dam worship as the ancestor of the family. After three generations, this &amp;#39;pu&amp;#39; part of the soul is liberated as &amp;#39;phi&amp;#39; or &amp;#39;god&amp;#39; and is considered a deity worthy of worship in worship such as &amp;#39;um-fa&amp;#39; and &amp;#39;chai-fa&amp;#39; The &amp;#39;Fu&amp;#39; or &amp;#39;Han&amp;#39; form of the soul is beautifully buried in a tomb with the body and given a place of eternal peace. The body is placed on a palki or shavadhar and buried in the ground and a strong wall of coconut trees is erected around the grave. This is</content>

There is a folk belief that the &amp;#39;fu or &amp;#39;han&amp;#39; form of the soul merges with nature after a few years. That is why family members hope that the spirit will be pleased with them and leave this earth when they bury the deceased. <content>Ahom Religion 183 Public Me-Dam-Me-Fi In almost all the cultured societies of the world, the ancestors are worshiped with their own characteristics and dignity. The Ahom, who came from the Mu Maolung region of southern China in 1228 AD, are no exception. Although their relatives in southern China, mainly the people living in the present-day Dihong-Dai-Singphou region of southern China, who accompanied Prince Chao Lung Chu-ka-pha in 1228 AD and settled in Assam, are known as Tai Ahom hall. The Ahom worship of the ancestors is called &amp;#39;Me-Dam-Me-Phi&amp;#39;. &amp;#39;Me&amp;#39; means worship, &amp;#39;Dam&amp;#39; means dead and &amp;#39;Phi&amp;#39; means god. This means that the Ahoms worship the dead as gods. The concept of divinity of the dead is very ancient and legend has it that all people of the Tai language have their own characteristics and traditions of worshiping their ancestors as gods. For example, the Dong (Kam) people living in Nan Guizhon Province and Guangxi Autonomous Region practice ancestor worship in their own way. The concept of ancestor worship is associated with the concept of immortality of the soul. After a person dies, his soul leaves the body and returns to the abode of his ancestors (ie, heaven). This is folk belief. Many families worship &amp;#39;heaven, earth, ancestors and gurus&amp;#39; in the main room of their homes. When people receive a gift from others or a newly produced product, they first offer it to their ancestors and then eat it. - The Tai Ahom believe that when a person dies, he remains a ghost and that the deceased becomes a &amp;#39;phi&amp;#39; or god only after the relatives of the deceased worship the gods and goddesses. According to Tai-Ahom belief, the human soul is not reborn after death. After death, the deceased person becomes a god and a heaven</content>

</page>

<page>
<page_no> 184  </page_no>
<content>They live with their relatives. They believe that if relatives or the whole society as a whole worships the ancestors (Me-Dam-Me-Fi) with devotion, the dead, especially parents and grandparents, come down from heaven to earth and wish their sons, daughters, grandchildren and others He gives blessings. Like the Tai-Ahom, the Red Tais of Vietnam believe that heaven is divided into different levels; Each level is again divided into several provinces and each clan or tribe has its own residence, village or town. Similarly, in the capital of heaven, the King of Heaven, Po-Then-Kham, lives with other gods and goddesses. Every god and goddess has &amp;#39;phi&amp;#39; or souls under him. One of the most important customs among the Ahom is that they do not cremate the dead (ie, do not burn the coffin); They put him in a coffin and buried him. This is called the &amp;#39;maidam&amp;#39; This custom is also practiced among other Thais, such as the Tho people of northern Vietnam. They perform the worship of the dead once every 49 days and once every 100 days. It is said in Ahom history that when the princes Khun-Lung and Khun-Lai were sent to earth from heaven where the god Landon reigned, the goddess Jachingphai advised them: &amp;#39;Your grandfather Lord Landon has sent you to earth to rule on earth. When the holy &amp;#39;Chinkara&amp;#39; flowers bloom in the month of &amp;#39;Din Ha&amp;#39; (Chat month), choose an auspicious day of that month and worship them by sacrificing animals and birds and offering other sweet things to appease Lord Landon and other gods and goddesses. Landon and eight hundred thousand gods and goddesses will come down to earth and bless you. This remained Landon&amp;#39;s motto for the Tai people and the Tai king. They believe that they are born in heaven; and they were sent down to earth before the princes descended to earth by the golden ladder.</content>

<content>Religion of the Ahom 185 It is also written in the history that the first Swargadeo (heavenly king) of the Tai-Ahoms, Chaolung Su-ka-fai, celebrated this sacred festival of Me-dam-me-fi and prayed for the blessings of his ancestors in his new palace. After his death, Chaolung Chu-ka-pha was buried in Charideo. His son became king and held the Madam-me-fi worship. Charideo is considered to be the Jerusalem of the East for all Tai-speakers as all the Tai-Ahom kings are buried in this holy place and the designs and carvings of the tombs have become the subject of research not only for all curious people but also for curious researchers. There are many Ahom kings in the country who celebrated the Me-Dam-Me-Fi festival every year. Only during the reign of Jayadeva Singh was Me-Dam-Me-Fi not celebrated. When the Mughal army under Mir Jumla defeated Jaidhwaj Singh, the king said to Prime Minister Atan Buragohain in deep sorrow and pain, “Gohaideu, the gods have abandoned me.” Then Gohain said, &amp;quot;Swargadeo, do not be sad. Victory and defeat are two sides of the same coin. <content>186 AHOM RELIGION This worship was performed several times. After crossing the Nam-kyu (Irrawaddy) river on their way to this country, Chu-ka-fai worshiped Me-dam-me-fi. By the end of the thirteenth century, almost all Tai people had become followers of Buddhism (the Hinyan sect). But even after their conversion, they did not abandon their ways. Non-Buddhist Thais, like Taidam, Tai Khao Nang and Nungkal in northern Vietnam, celebrate this ceremony by offering meat and other food items to the dead. Other Ahom kings also celebrated the Me-Dam-Mephi as an annual festival The festival was celebrated by the Dihingia kings Pratap Singh, Gadadhar Singh and Pramatta Singh. The folk belief associated with the Me-Dam-Me-Fi ceremony was echoed in the words of Chakradhar Singh, “My uncle Chu-Tamlai (Jaidhwaj Singh) could not receive the blessings of Fa-Nuru and other gods because he did not worship his ancestors Our soldiers are dead or humiliated.” This festival was celebrated with great pomp until the reign of the last known king of the Ahoms, Chandrakanta Singh. There are three types of Me-Dam-Me-Fi (1) public (for the king, state and people) Me-dam-me-fi, (2) dry Me-dam-me-fi (with five high bamboo altars &amp;#39;maihang&amp;#39;) and (3) another type of domestic Me-dam-me-fi (with three maihangs or altars). <content>188 Ahom Religion Umpha Puja: The Ahom kings performed Umpha Puja at least 16 times. The first time Umpha Puja was performed was by Chaolung Chu-ka-fai in 1253 AD when he established his capital at Che-tam-doi (Charaideo). On this occasion, he sacrificed two furry dogs under two bot trees to appease the god of the forest, Fi-hung. (Ahom History, 47). During the reign of Chaopha Chaupha Hit Pungpha, Umpha Puja was celebrated as follows. &amp;quot;Then the king summoned the Moru, Moching and Mochai Longs and ordered them to look for Mars in the chicken&amp;#39;s tail (bancheng). Accordingly, the priests examined the chicken&amp;#39;s tail and found the results favorable. A white bull, a cow, and many chickens were sacrificed to the gods, and all the gods of heaven were worshiped accordingly.” (Ahom Buranji, p. 356) - Three types of gods are worshiped in the Um-Fa worship. In addition to the gods of the Ahom religion (who are worshiped in the main puja mandap, the Bar Deoshal), the Hindu gods and goddesses are worshiped separately in the Hindu shal-ie Hindu puja mandap and the gods of the forest separately in the forest temple. The Ahom gods and goddesses worshiped in the main temples are (1) Khaokham (god of water and sea), (2) Ai Landin (goddess of the earth), (3) Jan Saisong (teacher and source of knowledge of the gods). Ching-Frang Hum is the symbol of the gods of heaven and the father of the Tai kings), (5) Ja-Chit-Fa (the goddess of knowledge), (6) Mut-kum-Taikum (the god of the moon and sun).</content>

Nowadays, Umpha Puja is held only every twelve years. Earlier, Umpha Puja was held every 3, 5 and 7 years at Hatibarua Deoshal, Bakta Deoshal and Amlakhi Rangagara Deoshal respectively. The Umpha Puja festival is a day-long event that lasts from early morning till late evening. Before the main ceremony, a separate altar (pang) is erected at the appropriate place according to the prescribed rules. A wooden pole called &amp;#39;kat-lak&amp;#39; or &amp;#39;yuk-chang&amp;#39; is also buried near the central altar. The cattle, pigs, dogs, ducks and birds brought for the sacrifices of the puja are tied there. Each altar is attached to these animal tie poles. It is noteworthy that the worship mantras of the Tai Ahom deity mention (1) the origin of the deity, (2) the sacrifices, (3) the names of religious acts, (4) the reasons for worship.</content>

<content>198 prepared the religion of the Ahoms. They themselves share the water of peace and sprinkle it on the offerings on the altars and on the animals and birds that are prepared for sacrifice. These mantras are actually nothing but prayers to the ancestors and gods. The mantra begins by bowing to the God of heaven as follows: 66 “Chao Nuru-Chao-Kao-Oi O Fi Oi Kiya Urai Ura O Chao Phra No Chao Phra Tekpin Ba” and so on. In the Ahom custom, the names of the gods are first invoked by chanting mantras. This is called Ao Tang. All the gods and goddesses are invoked and prayed to occupy the places designated for them. The priests then name the rituals of worship, the animals and birds prepared for sacrifice, and other articles of enjoyment, and invite the gods to accept them. This is called kin-tang. The priests then seek the blessings and mercy of the gods for the country and its inhabitants. This is called jan -ming. The Deuri then prophesied and ordered countermeasures to appease the gods and goddesses and prevent evil. When the Deuris dance in all three mandaps (deoshals), they sing Ahom mantras in the Bardeoshal and prayer songs in Assamese in the other two shals. While dancing, the Deuris drink the blood of the sacrificed goats and other animals and birds with their hands and make prophecies. The priests then sprinkle mantra water on the Deuris. Only then did the Deuris regain consciousness. The act of bidding farewell to the gods and goddesses is called &amp;#39;baitang&amp;#39;</content>

</page>

<page>
<page_no> 199  </page_no>
<content>It is called the religion of the Ahoms. After the &amp;#39;Baitang&amp;#39;, the Deuris fell to the ground, as the gods and goddesses with their bodies left them. Some time after this they regain definition (consciousness). (See Ahom Mantra of Umpha Puja in Appendix 3). Ahom mantras are not used in the worship of gods and goddesses in Hindu mandaps; Here, the mantras are recited only in Assamese. The worship is primarily aimed at the almighty Shiva (Mahadeva), the aged mother Subachani and their Pali-guardians. These include Ikola-Sikola, Yazli-Majli, Bidhi-Vidhata, Tamuli-Tekela, the goddesses of disease and epidemic, the goddess of spring (Saruai) and nine sister goddesses, Garia Raja, Maria Raja, Shel Konwar, Mal Konwar, Dhoba-Dhubuni, Panch Gharia. (See Mantras of Umpha Puja in Appendix 4). Legend has it that in the distant past there was no such thing as land or water. The moon and the sun were moving in their own orbits. The gods of heaven reigned in space. Four Siddhas built an earthen boat. The world appeared. But who saw it? Who cut off its navel? The frog crouched on the branch of the tree. The square beetle cut the navel. Brahma created, Vishnu maintained and Rudra destroyed. The gods of heaven were restored to their proper places. There was no power to destroy them. There were no gods to worship. Then the gods of human worship were created. The Great Mother, Lord Shiva and Goddess Subchani are approached to wish the welfare of all and to save them from various diseases, deaths and all kinds of evils (because they all happen because of our defection). (UMFA held in 2004).</content>

<content>The list of priests of 200 pujas is given in Appendix 5.) Ahom Religion One of the most important features of Ahom religion is that among all the rituals of this religion, only &amp;#39;Furalung&amp;#39; does not involve bloody sacrifice. The Ahom performed bloody sacrifices in most religious ceremonies. Only Furalung is an exception. Before giving a theoretical explanation of Furalung, it is necessary to say a few words about the preparation of Furalung ceremony in the Ahom priestly community and some other Ahom families. This event is celebrated in several ways. such as (1) celebrating the full ceremony; (2) celebrate most events; (3) Furalung celebrated by a householder for a special purpose; (4) A ceremony celebrated in a small family limited to the primary level. The following stages are compulsory for any type of Furalu event. - (1) a high altar (&amp;#39;lock&amp;#39;) equipped with seven bamboo poles for lighting clay candles; installation of &amp;#39;Pak Pen Ka Ka&amp;#39; on a high altar; (2) (3) &amp;#39;Ja-pulak&amp;#39; prepared ; (4) &amp;#39;I Ching Lao&amp;#39; means singing prayer songs. Another important point is that the food served at the ceremony must be completely vegetarian; Fish, meat, corn and liquor completely</content>

</page>

<page_no> 201  </page_no>
<content>Ahom Religion 201 is forbidden. No cooked or cooked food is offered. Sweets are made by mixing rice flour, jaggery, milk and honey. Rice mixed with soaked beetroot oil, neem and ginger is served with bananas, maize and other fruits and vegetables. Coconut is considered to be an essential resource in every religious ceremony of the Ahom people. Special emphasis placed on devotees (fufusing). This gives this ceremony a distinction among other Ahom religious ceremonies. These devotees invited here selected three Ahom priests to perform the special duties imposed by the assembled people. (a) Construction of the Furalung Altar: A place is first selected for the altar. The altar is made of clay or sand in an octagonal shape. In each corner, a banana plant is planted in a hole in the ground slightly away from the altar. It is recommended that sufficient space be provided for the seating of controlled devotees. The priests and their families sit on the west side of the altar. Therefore, there should be proportionate open space. On the east side, a high altar is built to house the book &amp;#39;Pak Pen Ka Ka&amp;#39; Plenty of space should be left for serving foods in pots or other containers. (b) Welcome to the priest (snak-chit) and invited guests (fuk footing): The head of the household welcomes the priest and devotees with coconuts, pan and some money on a brass plate and invites them to take seats near the altar. The family members</content>

If the family hosts a feast for the guests, the assembly allows selected people to cook during this time. (See Appendix-6 for the songs performed at the Furalung ceremony.) (c) Installation of the &amp;#39;Pak Pen Ka Ka Grantha: The priests now place the &amp;#39;Pak Pen Ka Ka&amp;#39; sacred book on the altar. There are many ways to get a job in the field of architecture, but the most important thing is to get a job in the field of architecture. The book is covered with a flowery gomocha. They can also be covered with flower petals or leaves. The gamocha is covered with three cut coconut chinkar flowers. A clay candle is lit on the right side of the Pak Pen Ka Ka. In addition to coconuts and drinks, a small amount of money is also given to the sharai. The priests who installed the &amp;#39;Pak Pen Ka Ka&amp;#39; bowed down and blessed the assembled devotees. The vessel is also given gold and silver. The priests chant the mantra by stirring the water in the copper vessel by decorating a palm branch with a chinkar flower and tying a piece of copper to it Ya Pulak is a mixture of butter, honey, ab milk, curd, jaggery and sugar. <page_no> 203  </page_no>
<content>The three first drank some of it and purified it. Some of it is then sprinkled on the altar and other rituals. It is then distributed among the devotees. (3) Lak Sajja (Stapped decoration for lighting candles) For the complete Furalong ceremony, 101 slum trees should be lit, and palm trees six or seven feet high should be cut down and buried in the ground to decorate the slum pillars. Bamboo is cut in different sizes on the columns of palm trees in seven rows from bottom to top and arranged in a conic shape from bottom to top. There is a three-faced candle at the top and four candles in the second row. There are 16 candles in each of these four rows from the third to the sixth row and 32 candles in the seventh and last row. This conical lantern is called a &amp;#39;lak&amp;#39; When the Furalung ceremony is celebrated on a small scale, only eleven candles are lit. In this case, a coin of land is paid. Clay candles are prepared with oil shalitas and lit on seven rows of slum pillars constructed as described above. The main candle at the top has only three wicks. The widely celebrated Furalung ceremony is conducted according to the &amp;#39;Nigami&amp;#39; custom; However, the ceremony is also performed according to the &amp;#39;Agami&amp;#39; custom. An equal number of kaldonas are placed in front of the &amp;#39;lak&amp;#39; (i.e. slum pillar) according to the number of candles and cut coconuts covered with chinkara flowers and some money are placed on it. Food and rituals are also placed in front of it and candles are lit with palm leaves. Once this is done properly, the &amp;#39;lak&amp;#39;, or slum theory, is ready for lighting the candle. The head of the family will light the main clay candle. The rest of the candles are offered by the priests and other devotees.</content>

<content>204 Bow to the devotees of the Ahom religion : Now the family members will bow to the devotees. The head of the household will explain the purpose of holding the furalung ceremony. (g) Prayer song: This is followed by the priests, devotees, family members and others gathered who sing the prayer song of God (I-ching-lao) and offer flowers to the &amp;#39;lak&amp;#39; when this song of praise is sung. (I-Ching-Lao is mentioned in Appendix 6.) 2) Family members bow and offer sharai: After I Ching Lao, family members bow to the devotees and offer sharai to them. The priests give the blessing. (i) Name singing: A priest then conducts the congregational prayer. This prayer is divided into three parts; First, the priests are bowed. This is called Mo Hung Mo Lik Molai. Secondly, there is a bow to God (fura) and finally a prayer. The first and second of these are conducted by the priests but the devotees also take an active part in them. It does not use nagara or rhythm. In this ceremony, the priest puts the names and the devotees clap their hands and sing the verse collectively. This becomes very interesting throughout the programme. (j) After the collective prayer, refreshments are arranged for the devotees assembled. However, fish, meat or eggs are not used. (k) Leak Crack: Next, the three priests read the scriptures. The high priest sings hymns to Lord Fura and Jasingpha.</content>

(n) After the singing ceremony, all the hosts kneel before the assembled people. In the absence of this ceremony, the bow after the Phura-nam is the last bow of the host. (n) After this, devotees and priests give blessings (jan-ming) to the family members to celebrate Furalung. (d) Afterwards, if the host arranges a feast, all those present participate in it. Afterwards, the priests and devotees were formally bid farewell by offering sharais as a sign of respect. <page_no> 206  </page_no>
<content>206 The Ahom religion has been considered to be a very important religious institution. Nowadays, this program is popular among all classes of Ahom families. The &amp;#39;Ahom Assamese En glish Dictionary &amp;#39;, compiled by Golap Chandra Barua, gives the word &amp;#39;phura-tara&amp;#39; as meaning God and the word &amp;#39;phura-tara&amp;#39; as the name of a spirit, the name of a God. However, this first dictionary does not give the meaning of the word &amp;#39;furalung&amp;#39; Fukan rightly says (in Furalung) that the scriptures relating to Furalong were limited to the Ahom priestly community (eg Deodhai, Mohan, Bailung) and were not available to the common Ahoms. It was only in the late nineteenth century when inquisitive Ahom scholars went in search of old Ahom scriptures that they discovered that the scriptures relating to Furalung were only with the Ahom priestly community. The Ahom Lexicon (Nandanath Deodhai Fukan, 64) gives the word &amp;#39;Furalung&amp;#39; as &amp;#39;religious scripture of the Ahom&amp;#39; The Ahom also celebrate the Furalung Bar Sabah without any sacrifice with offerings of beans, bananas and corn. The Chomdeu (Chumphra-rung-seng-mung) Ahom were not idolaters in their religious tradition. There is no mention of any idols in their religious books.</content>

</page>

<page>
<page_no> 207  </page_no>
<content>Ahom Religion 207 However, the worship of Chomdeu was limited to the Ahom royal family. There was an idol called Chomdeu in exclusive possession. Khun Lung and Khun Lai, the ancestors of the Tai kings, were sent by their uncle, King Landon of Heaven, to rule the earth from heaven. They brought with them many sacred religious rituals and sent them to Chomdev, advising them, &amp;quot;I have given you Chomdev. Never leave this idol. It is in a box. At the end of each year you must open the box You will have long life. No king will disobey you. I, the king of thunder, have sent you to earth. We have blessed you with Jashingpha and Lao-Khri.” Landon then told the origin of the idol: &amp;quot;In ancient times, Falaibet (God) sent this idol with Phabutrung Shangdam. It was under the influence of this idol that Shangdam became a very successful ruler. By worshiping this idol you will be able to rule your kingdom. You will see Chomdeu in divine dress once a year. I have given you Thaomung and Khrumungnyeu and you will never disrespect them Choose an auspicious day and take a bath, then put on clean clothes and go to the temple of Chomdev in the morning with holy water.</content>

</page>

<page>
<page_no> 208  </page_no>
<content>208 When you reach the temple of the Ahoms, you will kneel before the altar of the Lord and pay your respects. Then open the box and look at Chomdev. Take out the idol and wash it with holy water. You shall drink of the water. Then you put the idol inside the bus and hit the saffron. Do not let anyone else see the idol. I have given you this advice, and you must never disobey me.” Landon advised the princes to touch the ground with their heads and bow to Chumphrarungmung Lik Chumphrarung seng mung says that the Fry (the Creator) meditated on the idol or luminous gem Chumphararung Seng Mung and prayed: “O my Lord, my Lord! <content>CHAPTER XI Rules and Regulations Related to the Burial Tradition The Ahom tradition of burial is still followed in many areas. The three clans of Mohan, Deodhai and Bailung are the priestly clans of the Ahom. In this case, the members of the priestly tribe follow this tradition. When an elderly man or woman who started a domestic life dies naturally, the family, relatives and neighbors of the deceased are immediately informed. As soon as the news of the deceased is received, relatives, relatives and neighbors come to the house of the deceased. These people are usually divided into three groups to conduct the funeral. A member of the first group hangs a sheet at the door of the barn. Some people take a bamboo from a tree, cut four poles from it, connect it with four knives, put three palm leaves on top of it and put a roof or rava three or four feet wide and six or seven feet long in the courtyard facing northeast. The rover&amp;#39;s floor is cut into pieces and cut into four corners of a board or board. While some members of the group perform the above activities, other members arrange to bathe the body of the deceased. This member is usually accompanied by a son, if a woman, a younger son. This is the work of the son</content>

</page>

<page>
<page_no> 284  </page_no>
<content>284 called Dharota with the tradition of giving a tomb. The person doing this work holds a pair of scissors in his hand. However, when he has to do something by hand, he scratches the scissors on his waist. The other members are usually peers in the relationship of the deceased. In the case of a person without sons, a relative whose relationship with the deceased is a nephew or brother or uncle. This bathing is done indoors. The body is lifted from the place where some members breathed their last and placed on a pira facing east. This area is usually near the main door of the house. After the body is placed in the pira, the worker breaks a chicken egg on the forehead of the deceased and pours the seeds from the forehead to the feet of the deceased. Then the body of the deceased is washed with water from an eva jar with amdali in the mouth filled with water. The bath water is not sent out through the door but through the wall (as until three or four decades ago most people did not have concrete houses, they had bamboo and thatch houses. Now, however, the policy has changed a bit). After bathing, the deceased is stripped of all his clothes and ornaments and put on white clothes from the waist down without dying. 1 As I mentioned earlier, a member of this group hangs a sheet at the door of the barn. He brings a piece of rice from the barn and folds a sheet over the rice dollar and puts three parts of rice in odd numbers in between. This dola is first placed under the rova in the courtyard and kept on the roof of the big house until the day of tilni before the body is taken out. It is believed that if a scholar finds something of the dollar of rice on the day of Tilni, it is considered a sign of misfortune. After the bath, the body is brought out of the house. The body is brought out and the head of the deceased is placed on a table under the cover of the courtyard</content>

</page>

<page_no> 285  </page_no>

Relatives of the deceased can visit It is customary for relatives to offer incense and flowers during the funeral. After the last viewing, the body is covered with a white cloth and the ends of the cloth are wrapped in the left hand and placed under the body. After the above rituals are performed, the three main udhans are brought from the barghar, ie the kitchen, and placed on the right side of the deceased near the roof. However, if relatives and relatives of the village also bring rice, eggs, etc. to cook the rice, they are also cooked. These cooked ripenings are done by the scissors. A chicken is also killed by a scissors, burnt in the fire, the chicken&amp;#39;s esophagus is completely removed, a pair of banana leaves are placed on the maihang on the right side of the deceased and the chicken, rice and two boiled eggs are placed. In addition, two coconuts are given with chitkara flowers and two bags, these bags are given luk-lao or nam-lao. Similarly, under the maihang, a cheopat, a coconut with chinkara flowers, rice, an egg, and luk-lao or nam-lao are placed on a plate. This whole given task is handed over by the holder. Maihang means raw palm leaves cut into inches and placed on four bamboo poles like four pillars of the sea table. A ceiling of one square foot is placed on top of this pole. <content>286 concentrate on making coffins with the tradition of tomb walls. They are usually made of four bamboo poles, similar to a wheelbarrow, but not like a ceiling like a wheelbarrow. The length and depth of the thief depends on the body shape of the deceased. Rangbhar consists of two bamboo or wooden poles but the two poles are not equal in size, they are unequal. Or the rods are given equal sizes back and forth so that the rangbharis do not have difficulty walking on the journey while carrying the loads. Now it is time to put the body in the tunkhong. On this mountain, before the body is placed in the tunkhong, four coconuts are placed on the body covered with cloth under the roof and the flower used is the kapat flower. Four coconuts are placed on one forehead, two on two arms and one on the navel. This is done before giving the coconut or the navel is cut. Navel cutting means that the white cloth wrapped around the navel of the deceased is filled with bamboo thread and tied with three female knots on the navel. After knotting, the remaining yarn is cut with a bamboo stick. The rest of the yarn is thrown on the roof of the house along with the shalar. This navel-cutting symbolizes the breaking of the relationship between the living and the deceased while they are alive, paving the way for a new relationship between the living and the dam. After the navel is cut, the body is ready to be placed in the Rangbhar. Now the jar used to wash the body is washed by sprinkling water from front to back with amdali. After the body is filled, two-story bow-shaped circular bamboo kami is placed on top of the rangbhar. This is called Kamik Chitika. Care is taken to ensure that the cloth does not fall off while carrying the rangbhar. This cloth is called morseoba. Beating a chicken means that a priest, who is not of the same family as the deceased, will kill the chicken on the shoulder of the deceased and after death he will rub one tail of the chicken on one shoulder of the deceased They put the rest in a secluded place. This beating signifies that the deceased has broken his relationship with the living from today and that a new relationship between the living and the dam has been formed. <content>Ahom wedding system – Chaklong - Ahom wedding system Chaklong is a unique social ceremony that reflects the uniqueness of the Ahom people. Those who marry according to the Chaklong ritual can feel the importance of the wedding ceremony and are inspired to follow the advice of this Chaklong ritual throughout their lives. Since it is widely recorded in the holy scripture “Lit-Nang-n-Fa” that the marriage of the great Ahom god Landon was performed in the Chakralong manner, all Ahom have considered the Chaklong ceremony to be very sacred. Landon was married to the beautiful Nang Hong Fa at the royal palace according to the Chakklong ceremony. This marriage is the path of the Ahom. These rules are discussed as follows: (1) Joran: The necessary clothes of the bride are sent from the groom&amp;#39;s house on the 3rd, 5th or 7th day of the wedding as much as possible. They also bring two clay pots, mangoes, ground beans and garlic. Silver temi knives and coconut pans are brought to Gamonb. The groom&amp;#39;s mother and other women, accompanied by two or three men, go to the bride&amp;#39;s house</content>

The groom sends the wedding materials from the groom&amp;#39;s house to bathe the bride with honey and garlic on the wedding day and keeps them in the land tenure. Similarly, women bring honey, garlic and soybean oil from the bride&amp;#39;s house in a bucket of water from the ghat. Apply a small amount to the skin in front of the house three times. They are used to make a fine bowl of honey and garlic and add a little sweet oil to the bride and groom. Water can be poured from the jug and then other water can be poured. They are placed in front of the bride or groom when they are taken to fetch water. The wedding ceremony is held at the bride&amp;#39;s house two or four days before the wedding ceremony. The first auspicious ceremony is held at both the houses of the bride and groom The bride and groom offer coconuts and drinks to the people at their respective homes before eating the feast, which is offered to the nine male gods and scholars, priests and relatives. Vishnuban or life-giving ceremony Two or three molungs (priests) take a boat and offer coconut, beer, eggs, rice, etc. on the banks of a nearby pond, river or lake and chant mantras to Khao-Tham (water god). The priests cook the fish they catch and place it on the heads of the bride and groom and sing the prayer “Chao Nuru Chao Kao Oi” to wish the bride and groom long life. Then the fish is cooked and fed to the bride and groom.</content>

Their book is called “Tai Ahom and Stars ”. &amp;gt; 20 From the translated astrological texts we learn how the Ahom kings determined their fortunes based on the positions of various stars. For example, when Kham Mu falls in line with the Naga star, when Kham-Mu falls in line with the Small Elephant Constellation, with the Chatra-mukhi Star, or with the Mattress Star, or with the Stone Cluster Star, or with the Sky Star When it falls on the line, it is bad for the country, the king and the people. <content>When the moon enters the stars, there is a devastating war in the land. If the star rises in the west, the king dies in his palace. If the three-tailed star rises in the west, the king dies. If the Kham-Mu star is under the mouth of the serpent, the king is unhappy in that year. If Kham-mu star approaches elephant covered star then the king is unhappy. The Ahom scholars call the large star that rises in the early hours of the night Puvati Tara. When the star appears in the west, the larger star is called the Tungkham star. The translators of these three books accepted Kham Mung as very important in Ahom astrology and associated it with the Ras of Hindu astrology. The position of the most influential Kham-Mung in Ahom astrology determines the destiny of a person. The Ahom scholars believe that association with Kham-Mu snake, small elephant star, Chatra face star, elephant cover star, mattress star, and stone star brings bad results. These three books mention that the presence of Kham-yukh (arm) had a particularly ominous effect during the Ahom period. However, these books provide many ways to neutralize or reduce the egg effects of such conditions. All healing methods involve the worship of the gods. Building houses for Lakni and Ram Mung is one of these ways. It is also stated in these three texts that the motions of Lakni and Tham-myung are always opposite.</content>

</page>

<page>
<page_no> 322  </page_no>
<content>322 Ban The gods who thus spoke of the cycle of the yathi year in one of these three books prescribed a cycle of sixty years;prescribed the kham-myung. The chakra is called pan. Another chakra is the Han. It spreads from heaven to earth. Therefore, the Tai Ahom consider the stick-year cycle to be a gift from God. The translators also say: Kham-myung is not just the position of a planet or a planet (Rahu), it is an event. The Kham-Myung cycle, which moves against luck, is of great importance; This is because the presence of Kham Myung determines one&amp;#39;s fate. In short, among the four constants of the Ahom, Lakni, Kham-myung, Pi Pan and Pi-han are the basic numbers and positions. Laka or Narg is an important constant in Ahom astrology. The fate of a person is determined by the status of the Laka or Nagar. The position of the snake or Laka is written in astrology as follows: Dinching Dinkam, in the month of Disham, the tail of the snake is on the south and the stomach is on the east. The back is to the west. If the house is built and touched by the idol, the householder dies. If it hits the back, the first wife dies. Similarly, in the month of Dinshi, Dinha and Dik, the head (laka) is facing east; the tail is facing west and the abdomen is facing south; The back is to the north. If you take the house, it is better to put three pieces of meat on the stove and put it in three directions. Similarly, in Dinshi, Dipet, Dikau Laka, the head is to the south, the tail to the north and the back to the east. The abdomen is on the west side. If you take the house, it is auspicious to put three fish in the west in three directions</content>
